Wasiyyah — Bequest
beginner
10 min
Definition and Ruling of a Bequest (Wasiyya)
Defines and rules on the bequest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of wasiyya, identify its default hukm as mustahabb, and explain when it becomes wajib.

Core Idea
A bequest is a deferred gift, recommended by default but obligatory to discharge debts owed to Allah.

This lesson opens the Book of Bequests. You will learn the precise legal definition of a bequest, its recommended status, and the conditions that can make it obligatory or invalid. The passage also clarifies why the bequest is not always incumbent, contrary to some views.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
Identify the Arabic definition: 'tamlik mudaf ila ma ba'd al-mawt' (a transfer of ownership deferred to after death). Note the key elements: transfer (tamlik), deferral (mudaf), and gratuitous conveyance (tabarru').
The commentator critiques this definition for excluding discharge of obligations.
2
Determine the default ruling
The matn states 'wa hiya mustahabbah' – it is recommended. Understand this means it is not obligatory (wajib) and that reward is promised for doing it but no sin for omission.
Mustahabb in Hanafi terminology equals sunnah ghayr muakkadah.
3
Identify the exceptions and conditions
Two exceptions are given: (a) if the testator owes rights of Allah (e.g., zakat, fasting, hajj), the bequest becomes wajib to discharge those duties; (b) the bequest is not valid if it exceeds one-third of the estate (for non-heirs) or if made to an heir or a killer.
These conditions are further elaborated later in the chapter.
4
Refute opposing views
The text dismisses the claims that bequest is wajib for every wealthy person or specifically for parents/relatives. The first is rejected without basis; the second relies on a verse abrogated by inheritance verses.
Be aware of the naskh (abrogation) argument.
5
Understand the qiyas objection and response
Analogy (qiyas) would invalidate bequest because it defers ownership to a time when ownership ceases, similar to a deferred gift during life which is invalid. The Lawgiver permitted it due to human need.
This shows istihsan (juristic preference) overriding qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

تعريف الوصية وحكمها

الوصية تمليك مضاف إلى ما بعد الموت، وهي مستحبة ولا تصح بما زاد على الثلث،

الوصية

كتاب الوصايا إيراد هذا الكتاب في أواخر الكتب ظاهر التناسب؛ لأن للإنسان مبدءا ومعادا، والوصية معاملة وقت المعاد، فناسب إيرادها في منتهى الكتاب على أن لها اختصاصا بالجنايات؛ لأنها تفضي إلى الموت الذي هو وقت الوصية. والوصايا جمع وصية وهي في اللغة: اسم بمعنى المصدر الذي هو التوصية، ومنه قوله تعالى: حِينَ الْوَصِيَّةِ (المائدة: ١٠٦) ثم سمي الموصى به وصية، ومنه قوله تعالى: مِنْ بَعْدِ وَصِيَّةٍ تُوصُونَ بِهَا ﴾ (النساء: ١٢) وفي الشرع: ما بينه بقوله: الوصية تمليك إلخ. براءة ذمته

من حقوق

وهي مستحبة لأنها إثبات حق في ماله فلم يكن واجبة كالهبة والعارية وقال بعض الناس: واجبة على كل من له يسار، وليس بصحيح، وقال بعضهم بوجوبها للوالدين والأقربين بآية ،البقرة، وليس استدلالا صحيحا؛ لأنه

الله

منسوخة بآية النساء، وإنما تكون مستحبة إذا لم يكن عليه حق مستحق الله تعالى، وإن كان عليه حق مستحق تعالى كالزكاة والصيام والحج ،والصلاة فهي واجبة والقياس يأبى جواز الوصية؛ لأنها تمليك مضاف إلى حال زوال الملك، ولو أضافه إلى حال قيامه بأن قال: ملكتك غدا كان ،باطلا، فهذا أولى إلا أن الشارع أجازه لحاجة الناس إليها؛ لأن الإنسان مغرور بأمله مقصر في عمله، فإذا عرض له عارض وخاف الهلاك، يحتاج إلى تلافي ما فاته من التقصير بماله. (ملتقط من عيني، ملا مسكين، فتح) مستحبة أقول : الحكم بالاستحباب على الوصية مطلقا لا يناسب ما سيأتي من التفصيل في الكتاب من أن الوصية بالثلث للأجنبي جائزة، وبدون الثلث مستحبة إن كان الورثة أغنياء، أو مستغنون بنصيبهم، وإن كانوا فقراء لا يستغنون بما يرثون فترك الوصية أولى، وأنها لا تجوز للوارث والقاتل، فكان ينبغي أن يقال: الوصية غير واجبة، بل هي مستحبة أو جائزة ، اللهم إلا أن يقال: المراد بقوله : وهي" مستحبة" أن غاية أمرها الاستحباب دون الوجوب؛ لا أنها مستحبة على الإطلاق تكملة البحر الرائق: ۲۷۰/۹]
The Book of Bequests. Definition and Ruling of the Bequest. The Book of Bequests. A bequest is a transfer of ownership deferred to after death, and it is recommended and it is not valid in what exceeds one-third. The bequest. The inclusion of this book at the end of books is clearly appropriate because man has a beginning and an end, and the bequest is a transaction at the time of the end, so it is appropriate to include it at the end of the book, although it has a special connection with criminal acts because it leads to death, which is the time of the bequest. 'Wasaaya' is the plural of 'wasiyya', and in language it is a noun meaning the infinitive 'tawsiyya' (recommendation), from which is His statement, Exalted: 'at the time of the bequest' (al-Ma'idah: 106). Then the thing bequeathed is called a bequest, from which is His statement, Exalted: 'after a bequest you bequeath' (an-Nisa': 12). In sacred law, it is what he explained by his statement: 'a bequest is a transfer of ownership, etc.' (Mulla Miskin, Takmila). 'A transfer of ownership, etc.' meaning by way of gratuitous conveyance, whether it is in specific items or in benefits, as in the general commentaries. I say: This definition is not comprehensive because it does not encompass the rights of Allah Almighty and the debt owed by the testator. If the author had said: 'it is a claim of Allah Almighty and the servants which does not reach them [in life], or a transfer of ownership, etc.' it would have been better. It cannot be said that including 'or' in definitions is not permissible because real definitions cannot have multiplicity; for we say: if one intends to define the reality within the individuals, that is permissible as established (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9:268). The exoneration of his liability from the rights of Allah. And it is recommended because it is establishing a right in his wealth, so it is not obligatory like a gift and a loan. Some people said: It is obligatory for everyone who has wealth, but that is not correct. And some said it is obligatory for parents and relatives by the verse of al-Baqarah, but that is not a sound deduction because it is abrogated by the verse of an-Nisa'. Rather, it is recommended if there is no due right of Allah Almighty upon him, and if there is a due right of Allah Almighty upon him such as zakat, fasting, hajj, and prayer, then it is obligatory. Qiyas (analogy) precludes the validity of the bequest because it is a transfer of ownership deferred to a state when ownership ceases, and if one were to defer it to a state when ownership exists by saying 'I transfer ownership to you tomorrow', it would be invalid, so this is more fitting. Except that the Lawgiver permitted it due to people's need for it, because man is deluded by his hopes and deficient in his deeds; when an obstacle arises and he fears destruction, he needs to make up for his past deficiency with his wealth. (Selected from 'Ayni, Mulla Miskin, Fath.) 'Mustahabbah' (recommended). I say: The ruling that the bequest is absolutely recommended is not consistent with the forthcoming details in the book: that bequeathing one-third to a non-relative is permissible, and less than one-third is recommended if the heirs are wealthy or are satisfied with their share, and if they are poor and not satisfied with what they inherit, then leaving the bequest is better, and that it is not valid for an heir or a killer. So it would have been better to say: the bequest is not obligatory, rather it is recommended or permissible, unless one says that his statement 'and it is recommended' means that its utmost is recommendation without obligation, not that it is absolutely recommended (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 9:270).
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Bequests (Kitab al-Wasaya) and provides both the linguistic and legal definition of wasiyya, followed by its ruling. The author states the matn: 'A bequest is a transfer of ownership deferred to after death, and it is recommended (mustahabbah), and it is not valid in what exceeds one-third.' The sharh then explains the placement of this book at the end of the work, noting the appropriateness because the bequest is a transaction at the threshold of death. Linguistically, wasiyya is the verbal noun meaning 'recommendation', and it is used in the Quran both as the act of bequeathing and the thing bequeathed. Legally, the definition of 'transfer of ownership' is qualified by the commentator as being gratuitous, covering both specific items and usufructs, though the commentator (likely a later scholar) critiques that this definition does not encompass rights of Allah (like debts of worship) and recommends an alternative definition. The core ruling is that the bequest is mustahabb (recommended), not wajib (obligatory). The text refutes two opposing views: (1) that it is obligatory for every wealthy person, and (2) that it is obligatory specifically for parents and relatives based on the Quranic command in Surah al-Baqarah. The refutation states that the verse of al-Baqarah is abrogated by the verse of inheritance in Surah an-Nisa'. However, the ruling changes if the testator has outstanding obligatory duties owed to Allah—such as unpaid zakat, missed fasts, hajj, or prayers—in which case the bequest becomes wajib because it is a means of discharging those obligations. The passage then addresses a potential qiyas objection: a bequest is a transfer of ownership deferred to a time when the testator's ownership ceases (death), and if analogous to a deferred transfer during life (which is invalid), the bequest should also be invalid. The response is that the Lawgiver has permitted it out of public need, as people are heedless and may need to rectify their shortcomings with their wealth when death approaches. Finally, a marginal note (possibly from a later super-commentary) clarifies that the author's statement 'recommended' should not be taken absolutely, because the details in the book show that bequeathing less than one-third is only recommended under certain conditions (e.g., if heirs are wealthy), and that bequeathing to an heir or a killer is not valid at all. Thus the intended meaning is that the bequest is not obligatory; its highest level is recommendation, not that it is always recommended without qualification.

Key Terms
الوصية (al-wasiyya): A transfer of ownership (tamlik) deferred to after death, by way of gratuitous conveyance, in specific items or usufructs.
تمليك مضاف (tamlik mudaf): A transfer of ownership that is annexed or deferred to a future event (death).
مستحبة (mustahabbah): Recommended but not obligatory; the act is reward-worthy but not sinful to omit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a bequest become obligatory?
A) It is always obligatory for every wealthy person.
B) It is always recommended and never obligatory.
C) It becomes obligatory if the testator has outstanding duties to Allah, such as unpaid zakat or missed fasts.
D) It becomes obligatory only for parents and relatives.

Key Takeaway
A bequest defaults to recommended (mustahabb), but becomes obligatory (wajib) if needed to discharge debts owed to Allah.

Wasiyyah — Bequest
intermediate
8 min
Prohibited Bequests: Exceeding One-Third, to a Killer, or to an Heir
Lists three prohibited bequest types and their conditions for validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of prohibited bequests in Hanafi law and explain when they become valid through heirs' ratification.

Core Idea
Three bequests are invalid without heirs' ratification: exceeding one-third, to a killer, or to an heir.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on three types of bequests that are initially invalid. The commentary explains the hadith evidence and the exceptions for minors/insane killers and heirship timing. Crucially, the heirs can ratify these bequests after death, making them valid. Understanding these rules is essential for drafting valid wills in Islamic estate planning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three prohibited categories
Read the matn and commentary to list the three forbidden bequests: (1) exceeding one-third of the estate, (2) to the testator's killer, (3) to an heir. Note that each has a hadith proof.
The one-third limit is from the hadith 'al-thuluth wa al-thuluth kathir'.
2
Understand the exceptions for the killer
The commentary exempts a killer who is a minor or insane ('sabi' or 'majnun') because they are not liable for punishment. Also exempt if the killer is the only heir. Note that indirect causation (sabab) does not trigger the prohibition.
Prohibition only applies to direct killing, not indirect.
3
Apply the heirship timing rule
Heirship is determined at the time of death, not at the time of the bequest. A bequest to someone who is an heir at the time of writing may become valid if that person ceases to be an heir before death (e.g., birth of a son). Conversely, a bequest to a non-heir may become invalid if that person becomes an heir by death of an intermediate heir.
This rule also applies to gifts and charity from a sick person but is opposite for iqrar (admissions).
4
Recognize the role of heirs' ratification
For all three categories, the bequest becomes valid if the heirs ratify (ijazah) after the testator's death. Ratification during the testator's life is revocable; after death it is binding. Only those with legal capacity (adult, sane) can ratify, and ratification by some heirs applies only to their shares.
The text notes the intra-Hanafi dispute on the killer, which will be covered in a separate lesson.

بخلاف السبب؛ لأن السبب ليس بقتل حقيقة، فلا يتناوله. واعلم أنه يستثنى من كلامه ما لو كان القاتل صبيا أو مجنونا، فتجوز الوصية لهما بلا إجازة؛ لأنهما ليسا أهلا للعقوبة، أو لم يكن له وارث سوى الموصى له القاتل. وقوله: "ووارثه" أي ولا تصح لوارثه؛ لقوله عليه: "إن الله تعالى أعطى كل ذي حق حقه، فلا وصية لوارث"، ويعتبر كونه وارثا أو غير وارث عند الموت، لا وقت الوصية، حتى لو أوصى لأخيه وهو وارث، ثم ولد له ابن، صحت الوصية للأخ وعكسه لو أوصى لأخيه وله ابن ثم مات الابن قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية للأخ. والهبة والصدقة من المريض لوارثه في هذا نظير الوصية، وإقرار المريض لوارثه على عكسه، فيعتبر كونه وارثا أو غير وارث عند الإقرار له. (عيني، فتح)

إن لم تجز الورثة راجع إلى الثلاثة المذكورة أي الوصية بما زاد على الثلث وللقاتل والوارث لا تصح إن لم تجز الورثة، وإن أجازوا صحت وقال أبو يوسف: لا تجوز الوصية للقاتل بإجازة الورثة؛ لأن امتناعها للجناية، وهي باقية، ولهما: أن امتناعها لحق الورثة؛ لأن نفع بطلانها يعود إليهم، فتجوز بإجازتهم، ويشترط أن يكون المجيز من أهل التبرع بأن يكون بالغا عاقلا، وإن أجاز البعض يجوز على المجيز بقدر حصته دون غيره لولايته على نفسه لا على غيره، ولا معتبر بإجازتهم حال حياة الموصي ، فلهم أن يرجعوا بعد موت الموصي، ويردوا تلك الإجازة؛ لأنها وقعت ساقطة بخلاف ما إذا أجازوا بعد موته حيث لا يجوز لهم أن يرجعوا عنه؛ لأنها وقعت بعد ثبوت الملك حقيقة، فتلزم. (عيني، تكملة)
And it is not valid in what exceeds one-third, etc.: meaning the bequest is not executed in what exceeds one-third, due to his saying – peace be upon him – in a long hadith: 'One-third, and one-third is a lot.' And it is related in the hadith that he said: 'Injustice is among the greatest of major sins,' and they interpreted it as exceeding one-third and bequeathing to an heir. And His statement: 'Nor for his killer': meaning the bequest is not valid for the killer of the testator, due to his saying – peace be upon him: 'There is no bequest for a killer.' And by its generality, it includes the direct killer, whether intentionally or by mistake, as opposed to the cause (sabab) because the cause is not killing in reality, so it is not included. And know that an exception from his statement is when the killer is a minor or insane; the bequest for them is valid without ratification because they are not worthy of punishment; or if the killer has no heir other than the testator himself. And His statement: 'Nor for his heir': meaning it is not valid for his heir, due to his saying – peace be upon him: 'Indeed Allah Almighty has given every right-holder his right, so there is no bequest for an heir.' And being an heir or non-heir is considered at the time of death, not at the time of the bequest. So if one bequeaths to his brother while he is an heir, and then a son is born to him, the bequest for the brother is valid; and conversely, if he bequeaths to his brother while he has a son, and then the son dies before the testator's death, the bequest for the brother becomes invalid. And the gift and charity of a sick person to his heir in this regard are analogous to the bequest, while the acknowledgment (iqrar) of a sick person to his heir is the opposite; being an heir or non-heir is considered at the time of acknowledgment. ('Ayni, Fath) 'If the heirs do not permit' refers to the three mentioned: that the bequest exceeding one-third, to the killer, and to the heir is invalid if the heirs do not permit, and if they permit, it becomes valid. Abu Yusuf said: The bequest to the killer is not valid even with the permission of the heirs, because its prohibition is due to the crime (jinayah), which remains. But for the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): its prohibition is due to the right of the heirs, because the benefit of its invalidity returns to them, so it becomes valid with their permission. And it is a condition that the one who grants permission be capable of donation, i.e., an adult of sound mind. If some grant permission, it is valid for the one who grants to the extent of his share, not for others, because he has authority over himself but not over others. And no consideration is given to their permission during the testator's lifetime; they may retract after the testator's death and revoke that permission, because it occurred as invalid. Unlike if they permitted after his death, where they cannot retract because it occurred after the establishment of ownership in reality, so it becomes binding. ('Ayni, Takmila)
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn's statement that a bequest is not valid beyond one-third, to a killer, or to an heir without ratification. The commentary begins by explaining the hadith evidence: the Prophet's statement 'One-third, and one-third is a lot' establishes the one-third limit, and the hadith 'Injustice is among the greatest of major sins' is interpreted as exceeding one-third or bequeathing to an heir. For the killer, the Prophet's saying 'No bequest for a killer' is cited. This includes all direct killers, intentional or accidental, but not those who cause death indirectly (sabab). Two exceptions are noted: if the killer is a minor or insane (not legally culpable), or if the killer is the only heir. For an heir, the hadith 'Allah has given each right-holder his right, so no bequest for an heir' is the proof. The critical detail is that heirship is determined at the time of death, not at the time of the bequest. Thus, a bequest to someone who is an heir at the time of writing may become valid if the heir loses heir status before death (e.g., a son is born). The rule is the same for gifts and charity from a sick person to an heir, but opposite for admissions (iqrar) which consider heirship at the time of admission. The next section deals with ratification by heirs: for all three prohibited categories, if the heirs permit the bequest after the testator's death, it becomes valid. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf argues that the bequest to a killer cannot be validated even by heirs because the prohibition is based on the killer's crime, which remains. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the prohibition is to protect the heirs' right, so they may waive that right by permitting. The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn. Additional rulings: the person permitting must be of legal capacity (adult, sane); if some heirs permit, the bequest applies only to their shares; permission during the testator's life is not final and can be revoked after death, but permission after death is binding.

Key Terms
الثلث (al-thuluth): One-third of the testator's estate; the maximum amount that can be bequeathed without heirs' permission.
قاتل (qatil): The direct killer of the testator, whether intentional or accidental, but not an indirect cause (sabab).
إجازة الورثة (ijazat al-warathah): The ratification or permission of the heirs after the testator's death, which can validate an otherwise invalid bequest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a bequest to a killer absolutely invalid, even if the heirs ratify?
A) When the killer is a minor.
B) When the killer is insane.
C) When the killer is the only heir.
D) According to Abu Yusuf, but the relied-upon position allows ratification.

Key Takeaway
A bequest exceeding one-third, to a killer (except minor/insane), or to an heir is invalid unless the heirs ratify it after death.

Wasiyyah — Bequest
advanced

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Can Heirs Ratify a Bequest to the Testator's Killer?
Presents the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on bequest to a killer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the validity of a bequest to a killer after heirs' ratification, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that heirs' ratification validates a bequest to a killer; Abu Yusuf disagrees because the prohibition is due to the crime, not the heirs' right.

This lesson dives into an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a bequest to the testator's killer can become valid if the heirs permit it. The disagreement reveals a fundamental difference in the basis of the prohibition: is it to protect the heirs' right or to punish the killer's crime? The lesson also covers the conditions of ratification and the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling on bequest to a killer
Recall that a bequest to a killer is invalid without ratification. Now focus on the killer category and the disagreement.
The prohibition is from the hadith 'la wasiyyata li-qatil'.
2
Identify the two positions inside the Hanafi school
Abu Yusuf: bequest to killer is invalid even with heirs' ratification because the prohibition is based on the killer's crime (jinayah). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn): it becomes valid with ratification because the prohibition is to protect the heirs' right.
The text says 'lahuma' referring to the Sahibayn.
3
Analyze the dalil of each side
For Abu Yusuf: the crime remains regardless of permission. For the Sahibayn: the benefit of invalidity returns to the heirs, so they can waive it. Also the hadith's primary concern is protecting heirs, as in the one-third rule.
The Sahibayn use analogical reasoning (qiyas) from the one-third rule.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Yusuf's view, the bequest to a killer is absolutely void and can never be valid. Under the Sahibayn's view, it is suspended (mawquf) upon ratification; if heirs ratify, it becomes valid.
This affects fatwa and estate distribution.
5
Identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's position. The text indicates their view is preferred and the final ruling is that ratification validates the bequest.
This is the position applied in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تصح بما زاد إلخ: أي لا تنفذ الوصية بما زاد على الثلث؛ لقوله عليه في حديث طويل: "الثلث والثلث كثير"، وورد في الحديث أنه قال: "الحيف في أكبر الكبائر"، وفسروه بالزيادة على الثلث وبالوصية للوارث وقوله: "ولا لقاتله" أي ولا تصح الوصية لقاتل الموصي ؛ لقوله عليه : "لا وصية لقاتل" وهو بإطلاقه يتناول القاتل مباشرة عمدا كان أو خطأ،

بخلاف السبب؛ لأن السبب ليس بقتل حقيقة، فلا يتناوله. واعلم أنه يستثنى من كلامه ما لو كان القاتل صبيا أو مجنونا، فتجوز الوصية لهما بلا إجازة؛ لأنهما ليسا أهلا للعقوبة، أو لم يكن له وارث سوى الموصى له القاتل. وقوله: "ووارثه" أي ولا تصح لوارثه؛ لقوله عليه: "إن الله تعالى أعطى كل ذي حق حقه، فلا وصية لوارث"، ويعتبر كونه وارثا أو غير وارث عند الموت، لا وقت الوصية، حتى لو أوصى لأخيه وهو وارث، ثم ولد له ابن، صحت الوصية للأخ وعكسه لو أوصى لأخيه وله ابن ثم مات الابن قبل موت الموصي بطلت الوصية للأخ. والهبة والصدقة من المريض لوارثه في هذا نظير الوصية، وإقرار المريض لوارثه على عكسه، فيعتبر كونه وارثا أو غير وارث عند الإقرار له. (عيني، فتح)

إن لم تجز الورثة راجع إلى الثلاثة المذكورة أي الوصية بما زاد على الثلث وللقاتل والوارث لا تصح إن لم تجز الورثة، وإن أجازوا صحت وقال أبو يوسف: لا تجوز الوصية للقاتل بإجازة الورثة؛ لأن امتناعها للجناية، وهي باقية، ولهما: أن امتناعها لحق الورثة؛ لأن نفع بطلانها يعود إليهم، فتجوز بإجازتهم، ويشترط أن يكون المجيز من أهل التبرع بأن يكون بالغا عاقلا، وإن أجاز البعض يجوز على المجيز بقدر حصته دون غيره لولايته على نفسه لا على غيره، ولا معتبر بإجازتهم حال حياة الموصي ، فلهم أن يرجعوا بعد موت الموصي، ويردوا تلك الإجازة؛ لأنها وقعت ساقطة بخلاف ما إذا أجازوا بعد موته حيث لا يجوز لهم أن يرجعوا عنه؛ لأنها وقعت بعد ثبوت الملك حقيقة، فتلزم. (عيني، تكملة)
And it is not valid in what exceeds one-third, etc.: meaning the bequest is not executed in what exceeds one-third, due to his saying – peace be upon him – in a long hadith: 'One-third, and one-third is a lot.' And it is related in the hadith that he said: 'Injustice is among the greatest of major sins,' and they interpreted it as exceeding one-third and bequeathing to an heir. And His statement: 'Nor for his killer': meaning the bequest is not valid for the killer of the testator, due to his saying – peace be upon him: 'There is no bequest for a killer.' And by its generality, it includes the direct killer, whether intentionally or by mistake, as opposed to the cause (sabab) because the cause is not killing in reality, so it is not included. And know that an exception from his statement is when the killer is a minor or insane; the bequest for them is valid without ratification because they are not worthy of punishment; or if the killer has no heir other than the testator himself. And His statement: 'Nor for his heir': meaning it is not valid for his heir, due to his saying – peace be upon him: 'Indeed Allah Almighty has given every right-holder his right, so there is no bequest for an heir.' And being an heir or non-heir is considered at the time of death, not at the time of the bequest. So if one bequeaths to his brother while he is an heir, and then a son is born to him, the bequest for the brother is valid; and conversely, if he bequeaths to his brother while he has a son, and then the son dies before the testator's death, the bequest for the brother becomes invalid. And the gift and charity of a sick person to his heir in this regard are analogous to the bequest, while the acknowledgment (iqrar) of a sick person to his heir is the opposite; being an heir or non-heir is considered at the time of acknowledgment. ('Ayni, Fath) 'If the heirs do not permit' refers to the three mentioned: that the bequest exceeding one-third, to the killer, and to the heir is invalid if the heirs do not permit, and if they permit, it becomes valid. Abu Yusuf said: The bequest to the killer is not valid even with the permission of the heirs, because its prohibition is due to the crime (jinayah), which remains. But for the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): its prohibition is due to the right of the heirs, because the benefit of its invalidity returns to them, so it becomes valid with their permission. And it is a condition that the one who grants permission be capable of donation, i.e., an adult of sound mind. If some grant permission, it is valid for the one who grants to the extent of his share, not for others, because he has authority over himself but not over others. And no consideration is given to their permission during the testator's lifetime; they may retract after the testator's death and revoke that permission, because it occurred as invalid. Unlike if they permitted after his death, where they cannot retract because it occurred after the establishment of ownership in reality, so it becomes binding. ('Ayni, Takmila)
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn's statement that a bequest is not valid beyond one-third, to a killer, or to an heir without ratification. The commentary begins by explaining the hadith evidence: the Prophet's statement 'One-third, and one-third is a lot' establishes the one-third limit, and the hadith 'Injustice is among the greatest of major sins' is interpreted as exceeding one-third or bequeathing to an heir. For the killer, the Prophet's saying 'No bequest for a killer' is cited. This includes all direct killers, intentional or accidental, but not those who cause death indirectly (sabab). Two exceptions are noted: if the killer is a minor or insane (not legally culpable), or if the killer is the only heir. For an heir, the hadith 'Allah has given each right-holder his right, so no bequest for an heir' is the proof. The critical detail is that heirship is determined at the time of death, not at the time of the bequest. Thus, a bequest to someone who is an heir at the time of writing may become valid if the heir loses heir status before death (e.g., a son is born). The rule is the same for gifts and charity from a sick person to an heir, but opposite for admissions (iqrar) which consider heirship at the time of admission. The next section deals with ratification by heirs: for all three prohibited categories, if the heirs permit the bequest after the testator's death, it becomes valid. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf argues that the bequest to a killer cannot be validated even by heirs because the prohibition is based on the killer's crime, which remains. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the prohibition is to protect the heirs' right, so they may waive that right by permitting. The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn. Additional rulings: the person permitting must be of legal capacity (adult, sane); if some heirs permit, the bequest applies only to their shares; permission during the testator's life is not final and can be revoked after death, but permission after death is binding.

Key Terms
قاتل (qatil): The direct killer of the testator, whether intentional or accidental, but not an indirect cause (sabab).
إجازة الورثة (ijazat al-warathah): The ratification or permission of the heirs after the testator's death, which can validate an otherwise invalid bequest.
جناية (jinayah): Crime or transgression; in this context, the act of killing the testator, which Abu Yusuf considers the reason for the prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute on bequest to a killer?
A) Under both views, the bequest is valid if heirs consent.
B) Under Abu Yusuf's view, the bequest is absolutely invalid; under the Sahibayn's view, it becomes valid with consent.
C) Under both views, the bequest is absolutely invalid.
D) The dispute only affects the one-third rule.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position allows heirs to ratify a bequest to the killer, because the prohibition is based on their right, not on the killer's crime.

Bequests Between Muslims and Dhimmis
Rulings on bequests across religious lines

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bequests between Muslims and dhimmis, and explain why a bequest to a harbi is invalid according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Bequests between Muslims and dhimmis are valid by consensus; bequests to harbis are invalid in Hanafi fiqh.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq establishes the validity of bequests across religious lines between Muslims and dhimmis, supported by a Quranic verse and the principle of equality under the dhimma contract. It also highlights a key inter-madhhab difference regarding bequests to harbis (enemy non-Muslims), which the Hanafi school declares invalid contrary to the other three imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The text states two rulings: a Muslim's bequest to a dhimmi is valid, and a dhimmi's bequest to a Muslim is valid. Both are affirmed by consensus (ijma).
The dhimmi is a non-Muslim under Islamic protection.
2
Locate the dalil for each
For a Muslim to a dhimmi, the proof is Quran 60:8, which permits kindness to non-hostile non-Muslims. For a dhimmi to a Muslim, the proof is the equality established by the dhimma contract in transactions.
The Quranic verse is quoted in the text.
3
Note the consensus (ijma)
Both rulings are said to be by consensus, meaning all schools agree. This reinforces their strength.
Consensus is a primary source of law.
4
Distinguish the harbi case and the ikhtilaf
The text then states that a bequest to a harbi (enemy non-Muslim) is invalid according to the Hanafi school, contrary to the three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali). Understand the practical difference.
The harbi is not under Islamic protection and is considered hostile.
5
Understand the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi school restricts the Quranic permission to those who are not hostile. Since harbis are hostile, kindness to them is not permitted, as it may strengthen the enemy.
This is a contextual interpretation of the verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويوصي المسلم إلخ: أي تصح وصية المسلم للذمي وبالعكس أي وتصح وصية الذمي للمسلم، أما الأول، فلقوله تعالى: وَلا يَنْهَاكُمُ اللَّهُ عَنِ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يُقَاتِلُوكُمْ فِي الدِّينِ وَلَمْ يُخْرِجُوكُمْ مِنْ دِيَارِكُمْ أَنْ تَبَرُّوهُمْ ﴾ (الممتحنة: (٨) إلخ وأما الثاني، فلأنه بعقد الذمة ساوى المسلم في المعاملات، حتى جاز التبرع من الجانبين في الحياة، وكذا بعد المماة، وهذان بالإجماع، ولا تصح الوصية للحربي عندنا خلافا للثلاثة. (عيني، فتح)

وقبولها بعد موته أي قبول الوصية يعتبر بعد موت الموصي، ولا يعتبر ردها، وقبولها في حال الحياة، حتى لو قال في حياة الموصي: لا أقبل، ثم قبل بعد موته صح القبول عندنا، وقال زفر : لا يصح؛ لبطلان الوصية برده، فلا قبوله بعد ذلك، كالإقرار إذا ارتد بتكذيب المقر له لم يصح تصديقه بعد ذلك، قلنا: الوصية برده تمليك مضاف إلى ما بعد الموت فيعتبر الرد والقبول بعده لا ،قبله كما لا يعتبران قبل عقد الوصية، وكان ينبغي للمصنف أن يقول: "فيبطل ردها" بالفاء. (عيني، فتح، ملا مسكين)
And a Muslim bequeaths to a dhimmi, etc.: meaning the bequest of a Muslim to a dhimmi is valid, and conversely, meaning the bequest of a dhimmi to a Muslim is valid. As for the first, due to His statement, Exalted: 'Allah does not forbid you from those who have not fought you in religion and have not expelled you from your homes to be righteous toward them' (al-Mumtahanah: 8) etc. As for the second, because by the covenant of dhimma, he equals the Muslim in transactions, so that gratuitous transfers are valid from both sides during life, and likewise after death. And these two are by consensus. And the bequest to a harbi (enemy) is not valid according to us, contrary to the three (i.e., other imams). ('Ayni, Fath) And its acceptance after his death: i.e., the acceptance of the bequest is considered after the testator's death, and its rejection and acceptance during life are not considered. So if one says during the testator's life: 'I do not accept,' and then accepts after his death, the acceptance is valid according to us. Zafar said: It is not valid because the bequest becomes void by his rejection, so acceptance after that is not possible, like an acknowledgment (iqrar) if the confirmer retracts by denying the confirmer's right, the confirmation after that is not valid. We say: The bequest is a transfer of ownership deferred to after death, so rejection and acceptance are considered after it, not before, just as they are not considered before the contract of bequest. And the author should have said: 'So its rejection becomes void' with the letter fa (to indicate consequence). ('Ayni, Fath, Mulla Miskin)
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct but important issues: bequests between Muslims and non-Muslims, and the timing of acceptance of a bequest. First, the ruling: a Muslim may validly bequeath to a dhimmi (a non-Muslim under Islamic protection), and conversely a dhimmi may bequeath to a Muslim. The evidence for the former is the Quranic verse from Surah al-Mumtahanah (8), which permits kindness to non-hostile non-Muslims. The latter is based on the equality established by the dhimma contract in transactional matters, including gratuitous transfers during life (like gifts) and after death (bequests). Both are established by consensus (ijma). However, a bequest to a harbi (an enemy non-Muslim not under protection) is invalid according to the Hanafi school, contrary to the three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali). This is a point of inter-madhhab khilaf. Second, the acceptance of a bequest is only effective after the testator's death. Rejection or acceptance during the testator's life has no legal effect. Therefore, if someone says 'I do not accept' while the testator is alive, and then after death accepts, the acceptance is valid. Zafar (a Hanafi jurist) disagreed, analogizing it to acknowledgment (iqrar) where retraction after denial is invalid. The Hanafi response: a bequest is a transfer of ownership deferred to death, so the relevant time for acceptance is after death. The text concludes that the author should have phrased 'inabtalu raddaha' (so its rejection becomes void) with a fa to indicate the causal sequence.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim who lives under Islamic protection with a covenant (dhimma), enjoying rights and obligations in transactions.
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is not under Islamic protection and is in a state of war or hostility.
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of the scholars on a ruling; a primary source of Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a bequest from a Muslim to a harbi (enemy non-Muslim)?
A) It is valid, like a bequest to a dhimmi.
B) It is invalid, unlike the other three schools.
C) It is valid only if the harbi is a relative.
D) It is invalid only if the testator is in a Muslim country.

Key Takeaway
Bequests between Muslims and dhimmis are valid by consensus; bequests to harbis are invalid in the Hanafi school due to the Quranic restriction to non-hostile non-Muslims.

Wasiyyah — Bequest

Timing of Acceptance of a Bequest
Ruling on when acceptance of a bequest is valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that acceptance of a bequest is only effective after the testator's death, and evaluate Zafar's contrary position.

Core Idea
Acceptance of a bequest is only considered after death; rejection during life does not prevent later acceptance.

This passage addresses when acceptance or rejection of a bequest becomes legally effective. The Hanafi school holds that because a bequest is a transfer of ownership deferred to death, acceptance is only valid after the testator's death. Zafar disagreed, arguing that rejection during life voids the bequest, analogizing to iqrar. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) maintained that life-time rejection is ineffective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act: acceptance/rejection of a bequest
The text discusses when a legatee's acceptance or rejection of a bequest has legal effect.
Bequests are gratuitous transfers which become operative only after the testator's death.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
According to the Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), acceptance and rejection are only considered after the testator's death. Rejection during life has no legal consequence.
The text says 'qulna' (we said) indicating the majority.
3
State Zafar's objection
Zafar argued that if the legatee rejects during the testator's life, the bequest becomes void, so subsequent acceptance after death is invalid. He analogized to iqrar (acknowledgment) where denial before confirmation invalidates later confirmation.
Zafar's view is an intra-Hanafi minority opinion.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that a bequest is a transfer of ownership conveyed during life but suspended until death (tamlik mudaf ila ma ba'd al-mawt). Therefore, the relevant time for acceptance/rejection is after death, not before.
The author suggests the text should read 'inbatala raddaha' (so its rejection becomes void) with fa to show consequence.
5
Understand the legal principle
The key principle: 'Al-tamlik al-mudaf yattabit bi al-qabul ba'da al-mawt' (a deferred transfer is established by acceptance after death).
This distinguishes wasiyyah from iqrar which is an immediate acknowledgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And a Muslim bequeaths to a dhimmi, etc.: meaning the bequest of a Muslim to a dhimmi is valid, and conversely, meaning the bequest of a dhimmi to a Muslim is valid. As for the first, due to His statement, Exalted: 'Allah does not forbid you from those who have not fought you in religion and have not expelled you from your homes to be righteous toward them' (al-Mumtahanah: 8) etc. As for the second, because by the covenant of dhimma, he equals the Muslim in transactions, so that gratuitous transfers are valid from both sides during life, and likewise after death. Key Terms
قبول الوصية (qabul al-wasiyya): The acceptance of a bequest by the legatee; legally effective only after the testator's death.
تمليك مضاف (tamlik mudaf): A transfer of ownership that is declared during life but becomes effective only after death, such as a bequest.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession of a right owed to another; an immediate legal act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the majority Hanafi view, what is the effect of rejecting a bequest during the testator's life?
A) The bequest becomes void permanently.
B) The rejection is ineffective; acceptance after death is still valid.

D) The rejection is effective only if witnessed.

Key Takeaway
Acceptance of a bequest is only valid after the testator's death; rejection during life can be revoked by subsequent acceptance.

Recommendation to Bequeath Less Than One-Third
Advises bequeathing less than one-third regardless of heirs' wealth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended amount of bequest and explain the reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
It is recommended to bequeath less than one-third to maintain family ties, even if heirs are wealthy.

This short passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common question: what is the best amount to bequeath? While bequeathing up to one-third is permissible, the text explains that the recommended (mandub) practice is to bequeath less than one-third, regardless of the heirs' financial status. The reason given is that a smaller bequest strengthens family bonds and avoids imposing hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being discussed? What is its hukm level? The text states that it is mandub (recommended) to bequeath less than one-third.
nadb = mandub = sunnah ghayr muakkadah in Hanafi terminology.
2
Analyze the condition
Does the recommendation vary based on heirs' wealth? The text explicitly says 'whether the heirs are rich or poor' – the recommendation holds regardless.
3
Understand the rationale
Why is reducing the bequest recommended? The text gives the reason: 'because in reducing there is connection of kinship' (silat al-qarib).
The ethical goal is preserving family ties.
4
Contrast with the permissible maximum
What is the maximum allowed? One-third. How does the recommendation relate? The recommendation is to go below that maximum, especially when heirs are in need or even when they are satisfied.
This is a higher level of ethical conduct.

The reasoning given is that by reducing the bequest, one maintains family ties (silat al-qarib) and avoids burdening the heirs. The text adds that if the heirs are wealthy or satisfied with their inheritance, then a smaller bequest is better than no bequest at all. This is a general ethical recommendation, not a strict legal requirement.

Key Terms
ندب (nadb): Recommending (mandub) an action; it is reward-worthy but not obligatory.
النقص من الثلث (al-naqs min al-thuluth): Bequeathing less than one-third of the estate.

The Role of Acceptance in Wills: Hanafi Position and Dispute
Ruling on when legatee owns the bequest and exception if he dies before accepting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on acceptance in a will, distinguish between validity and ownership, and apply the istihsan exception when the legatee dies before accepting.

Core Idea
Acceptance is needed for ownership in a will, not for validity; if the legatee dies before accepting, istihsan transfers ownership to his heirs.

In Islamic law, a will (wasiyyah) is a transfer of ownership upon death. But does the legatee need to accept it? The Hanafi school holds that acceptance is required for ownership, though the will itself is valid without it. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning, compares it with the Shafi'i view, and examines the exceptional case where the legatee dies before accepting—where istihsan overrides analogy to preserve the testator's intent.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi rule on acceptance
Identify that according to the Hanafi school, the legatee owns the bequest only upon acceptance. Rejection voids the will.
This applies whether or not the legatee takes physical possession.
2
Analyze the dual nature of a will (inheritance and gift)
A will resembles inheritance (ownership vests at death) and gift (transfer by contract). Hanafis prioritize the gift aspect regarding acceptance, so ownership requires acceptance. After acceptance, the inheritance aspect allows ownership without possession.
3
Examine the exception: legatee dies before accepting
If the legatee dies after the testator but before accepting, the bequest passes to the legatee's heirs by istihsan, contrary to qiyas (which would void it).
The testator's side is complete; the legatee's death removes the need for acceptance, analogous to a sale with a condition of option.
4
Contrast with the opposing view (Zufar/Shafi'i)
Zufar (in one narration) and al-Shafi'i argue that a will is a succession (khilafah) like inheritance, so no acceptance is needed, and rejection does not void it.
The Hanafi response: a will is a contractual transfer, not pure succession, so acceptance is required analogously to a gift.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The dispute matters when the legatee dies before accepting: Hanafis give the bequest to the legatee's heirs; the opposing view would keep it in the testator's estate or distribute differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وملك بقبوله : أي ملك الموصى له الوصية بقبوله فإن قبل الموصى له الوصية بعد موت الموصي ثبت الملك له في الموصى به قبضه أو لم يقبضه وإن رد الموصى له الوصية بطلت وصيته برده عندنا، وقال زفر في رواية والشافعي: يملك بدون القبول، ولا تبطل برده؛ لأنها خلافة فلا يحتاج فيها إلى القبول كالميراث. ولنا: أنه تمليك بعقد، فيتوقف على القبول كالتمليك بالهبة والتحقيق في هذا المقام أن قبول الموصى له بالوصية ليس بشرط لصحة الوصية، وإنما هو شرط إفادة الملك للموصى له حيث لا يثبت الملك بالوصية قبل القبول؛ لأنها تشبه الإرث من وجه من حيث إنها تملك بالموت وتشبه الهبة من وجه من حيث إنها تملك بتمليك الغير، ولهذا تبطل الوصية بالخمر، وإن كان الخمر يورث فاعتبرناها بالهبة في حق القبول مادام المقبول موهوبا من الموصى له، فقلنا: بأنها لا تملك قبل القبول واعتبرناها بالإرث بعد القبول، فقلنا: إن الموصى له يملكها بعد القبول من غير قبض عملا بالشبهين بقدر الإمكان. (عيني، ملا مسكين) إلا أن يموت إلخ استثناء من قوله وملك بقبوله أي يملك الموصى له بقبوله، إلا أن يموت الموصى له بعد موت الموصي قبل قبوله الوصية، فإن الموصى له يملك الموصى به بدون القبول، يعني يدخل في ملك ورثة الموصى له استحسانا، والقياس أن تبطل؛ لما بينا أن الملك موقوف على القبول، فصار كموت المشتري قبل قبوله بعد إيجاب البائع، وجه الاستحسان أن الوصية من جانب الموصى قد تمت تماما لا يلحقه الفسخ من جهته، وإنما توقف لحق الموصى له فإذا مات دخل في ملكه، كما في البيع المشروط فيه الخيار للمشتري إذا مات قبل الإجازة. (عيني، فتح)
And he owns by his acceptance: meaning the legatee owns the bequest by his acceptance. If the legatee accepts the bequest after the testator's death, ownership is established in the bequest for him, whether he took possession or not. If the legatee rejects the bequest, his bequest is voided by his rejection according to us. Zufar in one narration and al-Shafi'i said: He owns without acceptance, and it is not voided by rejection, because it is a succession (khilafah), so acceptance is not needed like inheritance. Our proof: it is a transfer of ownership by contract, so it depends on acceptance like transfer of ownership by gift (hibah). The investigation in this place is that acceptance by the legatee of the bequest is not a condition for the validity of the bequest, but rather it is a condition for conferring ownership upon the legatee, as ownership is not established by the bequest before acceptance; because it resembles inheritance in one respect (ownership by death) and resembles gift in another respect (ownership by transfer from another). Hence the bequest of wine is invalid, even though wine can be inherited. So we considered it like gift regarding acceptance as long as the accepted item is gifted to the legatee, and we said it is not owned before acceptance. And we considered it like inheritance after acceptance, so we said the legatee owns it after acceptance without possession, acting on both resemblances as far as possible. ('Ayni, Mulla Miskin) Except that he dies etc.: an exception from his statement 'and he owns by his acceptance', i.e., the legatee owns by his acceptance, except if the legatee dies after the testator's death and before accepting the bequest, then the legatee owns the bequest without acceptance, meaning it enters the ownership of the legatee's heirs by istihsan. The qiyas is that it becomes void because ownership is suspended on acceptance, so it becomes like the death of the buyer before acceptance after the seller's offer. The basis of istihsan is that the bequest from the testator's side is fully complete, not subject to revocation by him, and it was only suspended for the right of the legatee; so when he dies, it enters his ownership, like in a sale with a condition of option for the buyer if he dies before ratification. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage discusses the role of acceptance (qabul) in a will (wasiyyah). The Hanafi position is that acceptance is necessary for the legatee to own the bequest—it is a condition for ownership, not for validity of the will itself. This is because a will resembles both inheritance (ownership vests at death) and gift (ownership requires acceptance). Hanafis give precedence to the resemblance to gift regarding acceptance, so the legatee does not own until he accepts. However, if the legatee dies before accepting, the bequest still passes to his heirs by istihsan (juristic preference), contrary to qiyas which would void it. This is because the testator's will is complete and cannot be revoked by him; the only missing element is acceptance, and the legatee's death removes the need for acceptance just as a buyer's death during an option period does not void the sale. The passage also records the disagreement of Zufar (in one narration) and al-Shafi'i, who hold that acceptance is not needed because a will is a form of succession (khilafah) like inheritance. The Hanafi response emphasizes that a will is a contractual transfer of ownership, therefore requiring acceptance analogously to a gift.

Key Terms
قبول (qabul): Acceptance by the legatee of the bequest; a condition for ownership but not for validity of the will.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas (analogy) to achieve a more equitable result, here allowing ownership to pass to the legatee's heirs upon his death before acceptance.
خلافة (khilafah): Succession; the idea that a will is a form of succession to property, like inheritance, not requiring acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when a legatee dies after the testator's death but before accepting the bequest, what happens?
A) The bequest becomes void and returns to the testator's estate.
B) The bequest passes to the legatee's heirs by istihsan.
C) The bequest is given to the testator's heirs.
D) The bequest is divided equally between the testator's and legatee's heirs.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, acceptance in a will is a condition for ownership, not validity, and if the legatee dies before accepting, istihsan transfers the bequest to his heirs.

Will of a Heavily Indebted Person
Ruling with proof (talil) on invalidity of will due to encircling debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a will when debts encompass the estate, explain the reasoning based on debt precedence, and identify the exception when creditors release the debt.

Core Idea
Debts consume the estate; a will is a voluntary transfer, so a debtor with encircling debts cannot bequeath.

This lesson examines a key rule in Islamic inheritance: a person whose debts equal or exceed his estate cannot make a valid will. The underlying principle is that debts are prior claims on the estate, leaving nothing for voluntary bequests. We will explore the reasoning and the sole exception that can restore validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the hukm (legal classification) of a will made by a debtor whose debts encompass the entire estate. The passage clearly says it is invalid (batil).
The ruling applies regardless of the amount bequeathed.
2
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The passage gives two reasons: (1) debt takes precedence because it is a loan, and (2) a will is a voluntary donation (tabarru'). Debts are obligatory, so they must be settled before any voluntary transfer.
This reasoning is based on the precedence of creditor rights over the testator's wishes.
3
Identify the exception
The passage notes that if the creditors forgive the debt (ibra'), then the will becomes valid. This removes the impediment, leaving disposable wealth.
The forgiveness must be unconditional and before the estate is distributed.
4
Distinguish rights of people vs. rights of Allah
The passage mentions that obligations owed to Allah (like missed prayers) drop upon death, but debts to people do not. This reinforces that financial obligations to others are binding on the estate.
This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تصح وصية المديون إن كان دينه محيطا لأن الدين مقدم؛ لكونه قرضا والوصية بغير الواجب تبرع، وحق الشرع من الصلاة حقه ونحوها يسقط بالموت، فتكون الوصية كالتبرع، إلا أن يبرئه الغرماء، فحينئذ تصح. (فتح، ملا مسكين)
And the will of a debtor is not valid if his debt encompasses (his wealth), because debt takes precedence due to it being a loan, and a will for other than what is obligatory is a voluntary donation. And the right of the Shari'ah, like prayer and its like, is dropped upon death, so the will becomes like a voluntary donation, unless the creditors release him, then it becomes valid. (Fath, Mulla Miskin)
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage states that a person whose debts encircle his entire estate cannot make a valid will. The reasoning is that debts (dayn) are prior claims on the estate—they are obligations owed to creditors, akin to loans. A will, on the other hand, is a non-obligatory transfer (tabarru'). Since debts must be paid first, there is nothing left to bequeath. The will is therefore invalid ab initio. However, if the creditors voluntarily forgive the debt (ibra'), then the will becomes valid because the impediment (debt) is removed. The passage also notes that obligations owed to Allah (like unpaid prayers) are dropped upon death (since they are personal acts of worship), but debts to people are not dropped—they must be settled from the estate before any bequest can take effect.

Key Terms
دين محيط (dayn muhit): Debt that encompasses or equals the entire estate of the deceased, leaving nothing for bequests.
تبرع (tabarru'): Voluntary donation/gift; a will is considered a tabarru' that is valid only if the testator has disposable wealth after debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The will is valid for a tenth.
B) The will is invalid unless creditors forgive the debt.
C) The will is valid only if the heirs approve.
D) The will is valid until creditors object.

Key Takeaway
The will of a debtor whose debts encircle the estate is invalid; it becomes valid only if creditors release the debt.

Incapacity of Minors and Mukatibun to Execute Wills
Rulings on the invalidity of wills by minors and mukatibun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school deems the will of a minor and a mukatib invalid, and distinguish the three types of mukatib's will, including the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
A will is a gratuitous transfer requiring full legal capacity; minors and mukatibun lack it, rendering their wills void.

The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq establishes that both a minor (sabi) and a mukatib (slave under manumission contract) cannot make valid wills. The Hanafi proof hinges on wills being gratuitous transfers (tabarru') that demand complete legal capacity (ahliyyah). This ruling is contested by other schools regarding the minor, and internally among Hanafi imams regarding the mukatib's absolute bequest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories and their general ruling
The text states that the will of both a minor and a mukatib is not valid. Recognize that this is the established Hanafi position.
Minor = one who has not reached puberty; mukatib = a slave who has contracted to purchase freedom.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof for the minor's will
The Hanafi argument: a will is a tabarru', and a minor lacks the legal capacity for gratuitous dispositions. Any reward for charity is gained by leaving the property to heirs who may give charity.
Opposing schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) allow a minor's will for charity due to its benefit.
3
Note the temporal aspect: capacity required at time of will
Even if the minor later reaches maturity, the will made during minority does not become valid. Similarly, a conditional will contingent on maturity ('when I become mature, one-third is for so-and-so') is also invalid because capacity is lacking at the time of utterance.
This underscores the principle that capacity must exist at the moment the will is made.
4
Break down the three categories of a mukatib's will
First type: bequest of a specific item from his property – unanimously invalid because he has no real ownership. Second type: conditional bequest contingent on emancipation ('when I am free') – unanimously valid if he actually becomes free before death. Third type: absolute will made while a mukatib, then he later becomes free – disputed.
The third type is the site of intra-madhhab disagreement.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the third type
Abu Hanifah rules the absolute will is void because at the time of making the will, the mukatib lacked full ownership. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule it valid, considering the subsequent emancipation as fulfilling the condition of ownership. The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is Abu Hanifah's.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if he is freed before death, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad would execute the will; Abu Hanifah would not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصبي: أي لا تصح وصية الصبي أيضا، وقال الشافعي تصح وصيته إذا كانت في وجوه البر؛ لأنه نافع في و به قال مالك وأحمد ولنا أنه تبرع وهو ليس من أهله وما ترك على ورثته يحصل له الثواب، وكذا إذا أوصى، ثم مات بعد الإدراك لم تجز تلك الوصية؛ لعدم الأهلية وقت المباشرة، وكذا إذا قال: إذا أدركت فثلث مالي لفلان . وصية رمز الحقائق: ٤٧٥/٢] والمكاتب أي لا تصح وصية المكاتب أيضا؛ لأنه تبرع، وهو ليس من أ أهله. واعلم أن وصية المكاتب على ثلاثة أقسام: أحدها باطل بالإجماع، وهو الوصية بعين من أعيان ماله؛ لأنه لا ملك له حقيقة. والثاني: يجوز بالإجماع، وهو ما إذا أضاف الوصية إلى ما يملكه بعد العتق بأن قال إذا أعتقت فثلث مالي وصية لفلان، حتى لو عتق قبل الموت بأداء بدل الكتابة أو غيره، ثم مات كان للموصى له ثلث ماله، وإن لم يعتق حتى مات، أو مات عن وفاء بطلت الوصية. والثالث: مختلف فيه، وهو ما إذا قال: أوصيت بثلث مالي لفلان، ثم عتق، فالوصية باطلة عند أبي حنيفة خلافا لهما [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٧٥/٢]
And the minor: meaning the will of a minor is also not valid. Al-Shafi'i said his will is valid if it is for charitable purposes because it is beneficial, and Malik and Ahmad said likewise. Our proof: it is a gratuitous transfer (tabarru') and he is not qualified for it, and whatever he leaves to his heirs brings him reward. Similarly, if he made a will and then died after reaching maturity, that will is not executed due to lack of capacity at the time of the action. Similarly, if he said: 'When I reach maturity, one-third of my property is for so-and-so' — that is a will? (Reference: Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/475) And the mukatib: meaning the will of a mukatib is also not valid because it is a gratuitous transfer and he is not qualified for it. Know that the will of a mukatib is of three types: one is unanimously invalid — the bequest of a specific item from his property because he has no real ownership. The second is unanimously permissible — if he attaches the will to what he will own after emancipation, such as saying: 'When I am freed, one-third of my property is a bequest for so-and-so', so if he is freed before death by paying the kitabah or otherwise, then dies, the legatee gets one-third of his property; if he is not freed until death, or dies while having the means to pay (the kitabah), the will becomes void. The third is disputed — if he said: 'I bequeath one-third of my property to so-and-so' and then he is freed, the will is void according to Abu Hanifah, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/475].
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage covers the incapacity of minors (sabi) and mukatib (slave under contract of manumission) to make valid wills. The Hanafi ruling is clear: neither can make a valid will because wills are gratuitous transfers (tabarru') that require legal capacity (ahliyyah) which both lack. The minor lacks intellect and majority; the mukatib, though partially free, does not have full ownership of his property during the kitabah period — his property is considered owned by his master until full payment. The passage notes that Imam al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad allow a minor's will for charitable purposes, but the Hanafi proof is that a minor is not qualified for gratuitous transfers. Additionally, even if a minor later reaches maturity, a will made during minority does not become valid because capacity at the time of the act is required. The text also details the three types of mukatib's will: (1) bequest of specific property: unanimously invalid because he lacks real ownership. (2) conditional bequest contingent on emancipation: unanimously valid if he indeed becomes free before death; if not, it lapses. (3) absolute bequest made while a mukatib, then he becomes free: Abu Hanifah rules it void, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it valid, creating an intra-madhhab dispute. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifah's.

Key Terms
صبي (sabi): Minor child who has not reached puberty; lacks legal capacity for gratuitous contracts.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah (written agreement) to purchase his freedom by paying installments; during the contract he is part free, part slave, but his property is not fully owned by him.
تبرع (tabarru'): Gratuitous transfer/donation; a will is classified as tabarru' and requires full legal capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A mukatib says, 'I bequeath one-third of my property to Zayd' while still under kitabah, then he is freed before death. According to Abu Hanifah, the will is:
A) Valid, because emancipation cures the defect.
B) Void, because at the time of the will the mukatib lacked full ownership.
C) Valid only if approved by the master.
D) Void only if the master objects.

Key Takeaway
A will is a gratuitous transfer requiring full legal capacity at the time of declaration; minors and mukatibun lack this capacity, rendering their wills void, with only a conditional bequest by a mukatib being valid by consensus.

Will for a Fetus, Will of a Fetus, and Excluding a Slave-Girl's Pregnancy
Rulings on the validity of wills made for a fetus, by a fetus, and excluding a slave-girl's pregnancy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on wills involving a fetus (as beneficiary and as subject) and explain the validity of excluding a pregnancy from a bequest, using the principles of istikhlaf and the dual nature of wasiyyah.

Core Idea
A will for an unborn child is valid due to its inheritance-like character; a gift is not because acceptance is impossible.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on wills (wasiyyah) that involve a fetus (haml). While a gift to a fetus is invalid because acceptance and possession are impossible, a will is valid because it shares features of inheritance, which does not require the beneficiary's active acceptance. The passage also addresses bequeathing a slave-girl while excluding her pregnancy, confirming that both the will and the exception are valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three distinct rulings
List the three legal scenarios covered: (1) will for the benefit of a fetus, (2) will bequeathing a fetus itself, and (3) will of a slave-girl excluding her pregnancy.
These are all ruled valid under certain conditions.
2
Contrast with hibah
Note that a gift (hibah) to a fetus is invalid because acceptance (qabul) and taking possession (qabd) are essential conditions that the fetus cannot fulfill.
This contrast highlights the unique nature of wasiyyah.
3
Understand the dual nature of wasiyyah
The wasiyyah resembles both a gift (requiring acceptance when possible) and inheritance (not requiring explicit acceptance). For the fetus, acceptance is impossible, so the inheritance aspect prevails, and the condition is waived.
This is the key legal reasoning (ta'lil) for validity.
4
Apply the condition of live birth within six months
For the will to be valid, the fetus must be born alive within the minimum period of gestation (six months). The starting point for counting is disputed: from the time of the will or from the time of death.
This condition applies to both will for fetus and will by fetus.
5
Apply the exception principle
When a slave-girl is bequeathed except her pregnancy, both the will and the exception are valid because the pregnancy is not part of the name 'slave-girl' linguistically; it is a separate entity that can be excluded.
This is consistent with the rule that whatever can be contracted upon can be excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصح الوصية للحمل وبه إن ولدت لأقل مدته من وقت الوصية، ولا تصح الهبة له، وإن أوصى بأمة إلا حملها صحت الوصية والاستثناء. وتصح الوصية إلخ لأنها استخلاف، والجنين يصلح خليفة في الإرث، فكذا في الوصية، لا يقال: شرطها القبول، أهله؛ لأنا نقول : الوصية تشبه الهبة وتشبه الميراث كما مر فلشبهها بالهبة يشترط القبول إذا أمكن، والجنين ليس من أ ولشبهها بالميراث يسقط إذا لم يمكن عملا بالشبهين، ولهذا يسقط بموت الموصى له قبل القبول. (فتح) وبه إلخ : أي تصح الوصية بالحمل بأن قال: أوصيت بحمل جاريتي هذه لفلان؛ لأنه يجري فيه الإرث، فكذا الوصية أيضا؛ لأنها أخته. إن ولدت إلخ شرط لكلا الصورتين أي إنما تصح الوصية للحمل، وبالحمل إن ولدت المرأة ذلك الحمل لأقل مدته وهي ستة أشهر من وقت الوصية، وإن أوصى به يعتبر من وقت الموت. (فتح، ملا مسكين، عيني) ولا تصح الهبة له إلخ : أي للحمل؛ لأن الهبة من شرطها القبول، ولا يتصور ذلك من الجنين، ولا يلي عليه أحد حتى يقبض عنه، فصار كالبيع. صحت الوصية إلخ لأن الحمل لا يتناوله اسم الجارية لفظا، وإنما يستحق بالإطلاق تبعا، فإذا أفرد الأم بالوصية صح إفراده؛ لأن الحمل يجوز إفراده بالوصية، فكذا استثناؤه؛ لأن كل ما جاز إيراد العقد عليه جاز إخراجه من العقد على ما مر في البيوع، وعليه المتون، وفي "النهاية": من وقت موت الموصي، وفي "الكافي": ما يفيد أنه إن أوصى له يعتبر من وقت الوصية.
And the will is valid for the fetus and by the fetus if she gives birth within the minimum term from the time of the will, and a gift (hibah) for it is not valid. And if he bequeaths a slave-girl except her pregnancy, the will and the exception are valid. The will is valid because it is a succession (istikhlaf), and the fetus is suitable as a successor in inheritance, so likewise in the will. It cannot be said that its condition is acceptance (qabul) and he is not capable of it, because we say: the will resembles gift and resembles inheritance as previously. Due to its resemblance to gift, acceptance is required when possible, but the fetus is not capable; and due to its resemblance to inheritance, it is dropped when not possible, acting on both resemblances. Hence it is dropped upon the death of the legatee before acceptance. (Fath) And by it: meaning the will by the fetus is valid if he says: 'I bequeath the pregnancy of this slave-girl of mine to so-and-so' because inheritance applies to it (the fetus), so likewise the will because it is its sister? 'If she gives birth' is a condition for both cases: meaning the will is valid only if the woman gives birth to that pregnancy within the minimum term, which is six months from the time of the will. And if he bequeaths by it (the fetus), it is considered from the time of death. (Fath, Mulla Miskin, 'Ayni) And a gift for it is not valid: meaning for the fetus, because a gift has acceptance as a condition, and that is not conceivable from a fetus, and no one can act on its behalf to take possession, so it becomes like a sale. The will is valid because the pregnancy is not included in the name 'slave-girl' verbally, but it is owned by extension only. So if he specifies the mother by will, his specification is valid because the pregnancy can be made the subject of a separate will, so likewise its exception; because whatever can be included in a contract can be excluded from it, as was mentioned in the book of sales. And the texts (mutun) support this. In al-Nihayah: from the time of the testator's death. In al-Kafi: it indicates that if he bequeaths to him (the fetus), it is considered from the time of the will.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage addresses wills involving a fetus (haml). A will made for the benefit of a fetus (i.e., naming a fetus as legatee) is valid, provided the child is born alive within the minimum period of gestation (six months) from the time the will was made. Similarly, a will that bequeaths the fetus itself (i.e., bequeathing an unborn child as property, which was possible in the context of slave-girls) is valid under the same condition. However, a gift (hibah) to a fetus is invalid because acceptance and possession are required, which a fetus cannot fulfill. The reasoning is that a will combines elements of inheritance and gift; it resembles inheritance in that it vests upon death, so acceptance is not strictly required when impossible (as for a fetus), but resembles gift in that acceptance is a condition when possible. For a fetus, acceptance is impossible, so it is waived based on the resemblance to inheritance. The passage also rules that if someone bequeaths a slave-girl but excludes her pregnancy, both the will and the exception are valid. This is because the pregnancy is not part of the name 'slave-girl' linguistically; it is a separate entity that can be excluded just as it can be separately bequeathed. The time from which the gestation period is counted differs between the two cases: for a will to the fetus, it is from the time of the will; for a will of the fetus, it is from the time of the testator's death, according to al-Nihayah, while al-Kafi suggests it is from the time of the will. The commentary reconciles these views.

Key Terms
حمل (haml): Fetus or pregnancy; can be the object or beneficiary of a will under certain conditions.
استخلاف (istikhlaf): Succession; the idea that a will is a form of appointment of a successor to property, akin to inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man makes a will: 'I bequeath my slave-girl to Zayd, but excluding her unborn child.' The unborn child is later born alive. What is the ruling?
A) The will is invalid because you cannot separate mother and child.
B) The will is valid; Zayd gets the slave-girl, the child belongs to the testator's heirs.
C) The will is valid only if the child is stillborn.
D) The will is void because the exception is not allowed.

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, a will for a fetus is valid if the child is born alive within six months, and a bequest of a slave-girl can validly exclude her pregnancy.
