Rulings on Acceptance, Rejection, and Sale by the Executor
Introduction to executor rulings with acceptance, rejection, and acceptance by action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on acceptance and rejection of executorship and distinguish between verbal acceptance and acceptance by action (qabul dalalah).

Core Idea
The executor is a volunteer: acceptance is not compulsory, but once done in the testator's presence it binds.

This passage opens the chapter on the executor (wasi), following the chapter on the legatee. It establishes that the executor is a volunteer (mutabarri')—he is not compelled to accept the role. However, if he accepts or rejects in the testator's presence, his word is binding because the testator relies on it. If he remains silent until death, he retains the choice. Moreover, acceptance can be implied by action, such as selling the estate, which is treated like explicit acceptance. The passage also contrasts executorship with agency (wakalah), where knowledge is required. This lesson walks through these rules and their underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the executor's status
Recognize that the wasi is a volunteer (mutabarri'), not compelled to accept. The testator has no authority to force anyone.
Hanafi position: mubah (permissible) to accept or reject.
2
Distinguish acceptance/rejection in presence vs. absence
If the wasi accepts or rejects in the testator's presence, it is binding because the testator relies on it. Rejection without knowledge does not take effect, as the testator would be deceived.
The testator’s reliance is key; rejection must be communicated in his presence.
3
Understand the ruling when the wasi remains silent until death
If he neither accepts nor rejects before the testator dies, he has the choice (khiyar) to accept or reject after death. The testator’s absence of compulsion continues.
This contrasts with some schools that consider silence as rejection.
4
Recognize acceptance by action (qabul dalalah)
Selling the estate after death is implicit acceptance, valid even without prior knowledge of the bequest. This differs from agency, which requires knowledge because the principal’s authority continues.
Executorship is like inheritance: it applies after the testator’s authority ceases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوصي: لما فرغ من بيان أحكام الموصى له شرع في بيان أحكام الموصى إليه، وهو الوصي، وقدم أحكام الموصى له؛ لكثرتها وكثرة وقوعها، فكانت الحاجة إلى معرفتها أمس تكملة البحر الرائق: ٣٦٧/٩] ورد عنده إلخ لأنه متبرع في ذلك، فإن شاء دام عليه، وإن شاء رجع؛ إذ ليس للموصي ولاية إلزام التصرف على الغير، وليس في الرجوع تغريرا؛ إذ يمكنه أن يوصي إلى غيره، والمراد بقوله: قبل عنده ورد علم بالقبول والرد. (فتح) بزيادة وإلا لا أي وإن ردها في غير علم الموصي لا يرتد؛ لأنه لما قبل في وجهه اعتمد الموصي على قبوله، فلم يوص إلى غيره، فلو جوزنا رده في حياته أو بعد مماته لصار الميت مغرورا، وذلك باطل، وإن لم يقبل ولم يرد حتى مات الموصي، فهو بالخيار إن شاء قبل، وإن شاء لم يقبل؛ لأن الموصي ليس له ولاية الإلزام، فبقي مخيرا. وبيعه تركته إلخ شرع المؤلف يبين أن القبول تارة يكون باللفظ، وتارة يكون بالفعل، فالقبول بالفعل بأن التركة قبل القبول باللفظ، فهو قبول دلالة، وهو معتبر بالموت وينفذ البيع لصدوره من الوصي، سواء علم بالإيصاء أو لم يعلم بخلاف الوكيل حيث لا يكون وكيلا من غير علم؛ لأن التوكيل إنابة في حال قيام ولاية الموكل ولا يصح من غير علم كإثبات الملك في البيع والشراء، فلابد من العلم، وطريق العلم به أن واحد من أهل التمييز، وقد تقدم بيانه. أما الإيصاء فخلافه؛ لأنه مختص بحال انقطاع ولاية الميت، فلا يتوقف على العلم كالوراثة.
Chapter of the executor: When he finished explaining the rulings of the legatee, he began explaining the rulings of the person appointed as executor, who is the wasi, and he gave precedence to the rulings of the legatee due to their abundance and frequent occurrence, so the need to know them was greater. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 9/367] 'And if he accepts in his presence and rejects in his presence, it is valid, otherwise not' etc. because he is a volunteer in that matter. If he wishes, he may continue, and if he wishes, he may withdraw, since the testator has no authority to compel action upon another, and there is no deception in withdrawal because he can appoint another. The meaning of his statement: 'accepts in his presence and rejects' is knowledge of acceptance and rejection. (Fath) With addition: 'Otherwise, no' meaning if he rejects it without the testator's knowledge, it does not take effect, because when he accepted in his presence, the testator relied on his acceptance and did not appoint another. So if we permitted his rejection during his life or after his death, the deceased would be deceived, and that is invalid. If he neither accepted nor rejected until the testator died, he has the choice: if he wishes, he may accept; if he wishes, he may not accept, because the testator has no authority to compel, so he remains with the choice. (Fath, Hidayah) 'And his sale of his estate is like his acceptance' etc. The author clarifies that acceptance is sometimes verbal and sometimes by action. Acceptance by action is [when] the estate [is sold] before verbal acceptance, so it is acceptance by indication, and it is considered valid upon death, and the sale is executed because it came from the executor, whether he knew of the bequest or not, unlike the agent who is not an agent without knowledge, because agency is deputization during the continuance of the principal's authority and is not valid without knowledge, like establishing ownership in sale and purchase, so knowledge is necessary, and the way to know it is that one person of discernment informs him, and this has been explained previously. As for bequest [of executorship], it is different because it is specific to the situation of the cessation of the deceased's authority, so it does not depend on knowledge, like inheritance.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on the executor (wasi). It first explains the logical order: because rulings on the legatee (musa lahu) are more frequent, they were discussed first. The main ruling is that the executor is a volunteer (mutabarri'); he is not compelled to accept. If he accepts or rejects in the testator's presence, it is binding because the testator relies on his word. If rejection is done without the testator's knowledge, it does not take effect, as the testator would be misled. If the executor remains silent until the testator's death, he has the option to accept or reject after death. Acceptance can also be by action: selling the estate after the testator's death is considered acceptance by indication (qabul dalalah), valid even without prior knowledge of the bequest. This differs from agency (wakalah), which requires knowledge because the principal's authority continues. Executorship applies after the testator's authority ends, like inheritance.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): The person appointed by the testator to manage the estate after death.
قبول دلالة (qabul dalalah): Acceptance by action or indication, such as selling the estate, which implies acceptance of executorship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an executor remains silent and neither accepts nor rejects until the testator dies, what is his status?
A) He is automatically bound to act as executor.
B) He has the choice to accept or reject after death.
C) He is disqualified from being executor.
D) He must seek the judge's permission.

Key Takeaway
The executor is a volunteer; his acceptance or rejection is binding only if done in the testator's presence.
